    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app',

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer5677076738930633019">A longtime spokesman for conservation, common sense, and sustainable agriculture, Wendell Berry writes eloquently in several styles and methods. Among other literary forms, he is a poet of great clarity and sureness. His love of language and his care for its music are matched only by his fidelity to the subjects he has written of during his first twenty-five years of work:</span>
  <span id="freeText5677076738930633019" style="display:none">A longtime spokesman for conservation, common sense, and sustainable agriculture, Wendell Berry writes eloquently in several styles and methods. Among other literary forms, he is a poet of great clarity and sureness. His love of language and his care for its music are matched only by his fidelity to the subjects he has written of during his first twenty-five years of work: land and nature, the family and community, tradition as the groundwork for life and culture. His graceful elegies sit easily alongside lyrics of humor and biting satire. Husbandman and husband, philosopher and Mad Farmer, he writes of values that endure, of earthy truths and universal imagery. His vision is one of hope and memory, of determination and faithfulness. For this far-reaching yet portable volume, Berry has chosen nearly two hundred poems from his previous eight collections.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5677076738930633019" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2056188625" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2056188625" />
    <a title="Ian Caveny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21216968-ian-caveny"><img alt="Ian Caveny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583164141p2/21216968.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2056188625?book_show_action=true">Aug 18, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian Caveny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian Caveny" href="/user/show/21216968-ian-caveny">Ian Caveny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21216968-ian-caveny?shelf=masterworks">masterworks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21216968-ian-caveny?shelf=agrarianism">agrarianism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21216968-ian-caveny?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2056188625" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3651751579574535992">Immaculate poetry from an earthy pen, if ever there were the culmination of the "high art" of words and the "low art" of farming, it is to be found here in the writing of Wendell Berry. Whereas Berry's poems is only the latest of his works that I have completed in the preceding months (following <i>Jayber Crow</i> and <i>Imagination in Place</i>, it is one of the first I picked up, catching a lonely used copy at the University of Chicago's library booksale one day last spring.<br/><br/>My encounter with Berry starts of</span>
  <span id="freeText3651751579574535992" style="display:none">Immaculate poetry from an earthy pen, if ever there were the culmination of the "high art" of words and the "low art" of farming, it is to be found here in the writing of Wendell Berry. Whereas Berry's poems is only the latest of his works that I have completed in the preceding months (following <i>Jayber Crow</i> and <i>Imagination in Place</i>, it is one of the first I picked up, catching a lonely used copy at the University of Chicago's library booksale one day last spring.<br /><br />My encounter with Berry starts off with a critical weakness: of all the literary forms of art, poetry is the one I understand the least. I recall how during MAPH a typical literary course would begin with a set of readings in poetry (say, Yeats for Irish Modernism, Baudelaire for Aestheticism &amp; Decadence, or Wordsworth for Wretchedness), and how feeble I felt approaching these texts. Poetry has this unique power to unman us, especially those of us who tend (as I do) toward analysis, critical engagement, etc. I quickly learned to treat poetry with a foreign type of skepticism, unsure of how I ought to engage, unsure of how I ought to read. For Christians, this often occurs when theologians and pastors encounter the Psalms or the poetic portions of the Prophets - they simply resist our rationalistic platitudes.<br /><br />But Berry eases you in, even if you are a foreigner to the world of poetry. This collection, well selected and edited, even eases you into Berry's poetic style, as his voice matures from 1957 to 1982. The early poems are often lackluster or overly-sentimental, but they still usher the reader into the world of Berry's Kentucky farms and rivers. There is a sedate holiness to it all that beckons like a lover, at first coy and shy, later passionate, later still mature and knowingly. One enters into a romance with the poetry of Wendell Berry.<br /><br />All the while, Berry is teaching his reader: he is teaching about farms and soils and weather, he is teaching about Creation, Fall, and Redemption, and he is teaching, perhaps most metapedagogically, of poetry, of the power of words, and of the purpose for a robust aesthetics. There is no room for those half-hearted expressionist poems that have clogged our ears for nearly a century, here Berry grounds us in the earth and in the world and fills our bellies with good, rich, nutritious food. One feasts at his table as a farmer does for "breakfast," before the day's work begins.<br /><br />To pinpoint any one exemplary poem would be a mistake; they all flow into one another (especially in the latter three subdivisions) with remarkable ease and beauty. The famous "Mad Farmer" poems are particularly poignant, suggesting both revolution and tradition in the same voice (thus, perhaps, the titular "madness"). Any and every reflection on marriage in general or Berry's marriage to Tanya in particular are diamond gems of poetry, far greater than any of the nonsense written on the subject by a wide variety of folks over the past centuries. Moreover, Berry's work, taken in whole, speaks to a world-that-is-not which dwells alongside the world-that-is, and it is sometimes difficult to discern which is which. Is this world we live in, full as it is of skyscrapers and strip mining, the real one? Or do we live in a fantasy after all (and a bad one at that)?<br /><br />This is also profoundly Christian work, of the caliber that no writer whose work gets sold at "Christian Family Stores" has ever touched, and it is more the pity, since I doubt Berry himself would be sold in such stores. (He is in good company, as several profoundly poetic Christian rappers are also not sold in those stores, and they represent some of the best poetry in the American Christian world...) The whole thing hums with the Holy Spirit and makes for a good companion (as I have suggested <a href="https://theologianslibrary.com/2017/08/11/imagination-and-the-world/" rel="nofollow noopener">elsewhere</a>) to theologian Walter Brueggemann's landmark <i>The Prophetic Imagination</i>.<br /><br />Other than being a masterpiece, I will add that this collection of Berry's poems has encouraged and inspired me to begin reading more poetry, a genre that, as I mentioned earlier, I had left very much alone until now. Now, I look forward to diving into the mess and beauty of these glorious words.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3651751579574535992" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2255579868" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2255579868" />
    <a title="Kirby Whitehead" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13329787-kirby-whitehead"><img alt="Kirby Whitehead" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349202760p2/13329787.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2255579868?book_show_action=true">Aug 23, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kirby Whitehead" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kirby Whitehead" href="/user/show/13329787-kirby-whitehead">Kirby Whitehead</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2255579868" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4557007375595873078">Wendell Berry writes with a weight. Sometimes it is so great I feel the page struggle to keep the ink from falling through into the ground, other times in the same poem, and even line, I watch the page struggle to prevent the letters from taking flight around the room. His perspective zooms me in to see the cleaving of dirt by the roots of the trees and spring flowers, and then I am rushed out to view the fractal dance of all life, the dead and living flowing in circles of desire, departure, ret</span>
  <span id="freeText4557007375595873078" style="display:none">Wendell Berry writes with a weight. Sometimes it is so great I feel the page struggle to keep the ink from falling through into the ground, other times in the same poem, and even line, I watch the page struggle to prevent the letters from taking flight around the room. His perspective zooms me in to see the cleaving of dirt by the roots of the trees and spring flowers, and then I am rushed out to view the fractal dance of all life, the dead and living flowing in circles of desire, departure, return and rest. Work is given its proper value, and hued with shades of urgent cantankerousness, and joyous duty. Rest is found in the strangest places, which when considered, give peace to brooding theologies. I am thankful for Berry’s earnest curiosity, and disciplined devotion to participation in life. He does it well. It is a gift that he records it so deftly. I am sure he knows and doesn’t know he is my friend.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4557007375595873078" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_24642132" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/24642132" />
    <a title="Max" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/152310-max"><img alt="Max" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1195486346p2/152310.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/24642132?book_show_action=true">Jun 16, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Max" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Max" href="/user/show/152310-max">Max</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer24642132" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11337018638585985302">Rarely am I so impressed by poetry that I read a collection cover to cover; this collection of Wendell Berry's poems (selected from several previously published volumes) is a remarkable exception.<br/><br/>Berry has earned some renown as an essayist, and I first became familiar with him in that role. What struck me most about his essays, however, was neither the clarity nor the construction of his arguments, but rather his capacity for meaningful observation and precise prose. These qualities translate be</span>
  <span id="freeText11337018638585985302" style="display:none">Rarely am I so impressed by poetry that I read a collection cover to cover; this collection of Wendell Berry's poems (selected from several previously published volumes) is a remarkable exception.<br /><br />Berry has earned some renown as an essayist, and I first became familiar with him in that role. What struck me most about his essays, however, was neither the clarity nor the construction of his arguments, but rather his capacity for meaningful observation and precise prose. These qualities translate beautifully to poetry, and insight and depth percolate up through Berry's every poetic description.<br /><br />For example, consider "The Sycamore":<br /><br />In the place that is my own place, whose earth<br />I am shaped in and must bear, there is an old tree growing,<br />a great sycamore that is a wondrous healer of itself.<br />Fences have been tied to it, nails driven into it,<br />hacks and whittles cut in it, the lightning has burned it.<br />There is no year it has flourished in<br />that has not harmed it. There is a hollow in it<br />that is its death, though its living brims whitely<br />at the lip of the darkness and flows outward.<br />Over all its scars has come the seamless white<br />of the bark. It bears the gnarls of its history<br />healed over. It has risen to a strange perfection<br />in the warp and bending of its long growth.<br />It has gathered all accidents into its purpose.<br />It has become the intention and radiance of its dark fate.<br />It is a fact, sublime, mystical and unassailable.<br />In all the country there is no other like it.<br />I recognize in it a principle, an indwelling<br />the same as itself, and greater, that I would be ruled by.<br />I see that it stands in its place, and feeds upon it,<br />and is fed upon, and is native, and maker.<br /><br />This poem conveys aspects of Berry's philosophy more powerfully than any essay of his that I have read: the importance of a deep attention to the individual rather than the abstraction of it (<i>this</i> sycamore as opposed to the idea of the sycamore), the interaction of the individual with its environment, and the importance of personal responsibility in responding to one's environment. By paying attention and responding properly to the events of our lives, we too can flourish in years that harm us, can rise to a strange perfection and gather all accidents into our purposes.<br /><br />Additionally, Berry uses the traditional tools of poetry like the professional that he is. Line breaks, for example, create powerful emphases and contrasts:"There is no year it has flourished in / that has not harmed it"; "It bears the gnarls of its history / healed over..." Lines are carefully constructed in striking symmetry: "Fences have been tied to it, nails driven into it, / hacks and whittles cut in it, the lightning has burned it." Anaphorae and assonant line beginnings (there ... that ... that ... at, in ... It ... It ... It ... In ... I) provide a certain rhythm that might be lacking due to the absence of any clear meter.<br /><br />Each of the poems collected here demonstrates this careful, lovely construction, and each provides a window into Berry's equally well-constructed philosophy. This collection is an essential read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11337018638585985302" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1900683335" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1900683335" />
    <a title="Jessica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/54399136-jessica"><img alt="Jessica" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514897779p2/54399136.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1900683335?book_show_action=true">Feb 07, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessica" href="/user/show/54399136-jessica">Jessica</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780865471894" href="/book/show/1569501.Collected_Poems">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1900683335" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12058529233400675061">Can I just say, I absolutely adore Wendell Berry's poems!?<br/><br/>It is fair that I do not like all his poems, but I enjoy over two-thirds of the collection. I didn't think I could identify so much with a man's words. It is as if he has spoken my heart on so many subject matters. Reading his poems is like reading a series of short stories. I would construct mental images after mental images based on his description, whether he is describing simple objects or telling stories of someone's life. Wendell Be</span>
  <span id="freeText12058529233400675061" style="display:none">Can I just say, I absolutely adore Wendell Berry's poems!?<br /><br />It is fair that I do not like all his poems, but I enjoy over two-thirds of the collection. I didn't think I could identify so much with a man's words. It is as if he has spoken my heart on so many subject matters. Reading his poems is like reading a series of short stories. I would construct mental images after mental images based on his description, whether he is describing simple objects or telling stories of someone's life. Wendell Berry effortlessly transports me into scenes erected by his penwork.<br /><br />His words are simple and unpretentious. He writes about the seeds, the land, the birds and the seasons. He expresses elegantly the circle of life, the hard work that makes a man and all the love that comes in between. Through his words, Berry demonstrated his love for the environment, his desire for peace, his longing for a simple way of life and, most impressively, his humility.<br /><br />I could feel his decency through his poems. I think he is a "salt of the earth" kind of person.<br /><br />At the time I read this collection, the U.S. was going through a lot of uncertainties and anger. I found comfort in this poetry collection. Respect the land by leaving the smallest footprint, work hard, do what is right, have faith... these are the themes of his poems. I wish people would adopt these simple principles. Then, romantically, I believe it would bring forth more harmony in the world.<br /><br />I definitely feel his poetry collection speaks of my own heart, or rather, how I aim to live my life. Even short verses about walking into the woods or tossing out some letter accurately reflect my thoughts and emotions associated with them.<br /><br />I would like to note that the first section of the collection reflects heavily on life and death (especially death). I thought those poems were words of an old man. I believe, though, Berry wrote them in 1964, when he was in his early 30's. To me, it was refreshing for someone to have such profound understanding of life at such a young age.<br /><br />| would recommend this poetry collection to people who desire to live by simple, rustic, old-fashioned principles. Perhaps nature lovers would enjoy parts of the collection as well. I give this collection 4.5 starts. The missing half a start goes to some of the poems I do not enjoy as much.<br /><br />Lastly, I am very intrigued about the rest of his work now... reading his poetry collection makes me want to read all his work (poetry, short stories, non-fictions) chronologically.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12058529233400675061" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_633355895" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/633355895" />
    <a title="Jasonlylescampbell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/156872-jasonlylescampbell"><img alt="Jasonlylescampbell" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/633355895?book_show_action=true">Jun 05, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jasonlylescampbell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jasonlylescampbell" href="/user/show/156872-jasonlylescampbell">Jasonlylescampbell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer633355895" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer815480462230219111">I was given this book seven years ago by a friend who still a regular part of my life. I was given this book at my 30th birthday party. In the inside cover I wrote down all the names of the people who came to my party. I can still look over this list and remember how much I enjoyed that part. <br/><br/>However, there is also some sadness as the great majority of those who attended I have lost all contact with. There are circumstances that explain this happening to some extent, but it is decidedly foreign </span>
  <span id="freeText815480462230219111" style="display:none">I was given this book seven years ago by a friend who still a regular part of my life. I was given this book at my 30th birthday party. In the inside cover I wrote down all the names of the people who came to my party. I can still look over this list and remember how much I enjoyed that part. <br /><br />However, there is also some sadness as the great majority of those who attended I have lost all contact with. There are circumstances that explain this happening to some extent, but it is decidedly foreign to the world that Berry brings us into contact with. <br /><br />There are many poems in this collection that were important to me, but I want to touch on two:<br /><br />This is from At a Country Funeral:<br /><br />But our memory of ourselves, hard earned,<br />is one of the land's seeds, as a seed<br />is the memory of the life of its kind in its place,<br />to pass on into life the knowledge<br />of what has died. What we owe the future<br />is not a new start, for we can only begin<br />with what has happened. We owe the future<br />the past, the long knowledge<br />that is the potency of time to come.<br />That makes a man's grave a rich furrow.<br />The community of knowing in common is the seed <br />of our life in this place. There is not only<br />no better possibility, there is no<br />other, except for chaos and darkness,<br />the terrible ground of the only possible <br />new start.<br /><br />This idea of valuing the past, of needing it almost logically if we are to go on living here in this place (vs. chaos and darkness) stands out as something missing in the "space age" or "mobile age", etc. And it seems to describe the burdens and struggles as we do enter into the chaos called novelty. I deeply want to understand my past and the people who have gone before me, literally my ancestors as well as the events of our nation that has led us to this moment. And I also want to enter my grave as a rich furrow for my own children, if that can be. <br /><br />From the poem Rising:<br /><br />The boy must learn the man<br />whose life does not travel<br />along any road, toward<br />any other place,<br />but is a journey back and forth<br />in rows, and in the rounds<br />of years. His journey's end<br />is no place of ease, but the farm<br />itself, the place day labor<br />starts from, journeys in,<br />returns to: the fields<br />whose past and potency are one.<br /><br />Though I will not be a farmer and must journey mostly on keys and through number fields, beneath this surface is a view of the world. This is from his collection called Wheel where he allows the story of progress (the idea of linear growth, often attributed to the west and founded in the Christian story) to come into conflict with the older idea of a wheel of life or a circle/cycle of seasons (also deeply embedded in the Christian story). Do we see our life as an epic journey, onward and upward or do we see it in the domestic reality of "back and forth"? Do we see retirement as the goal, the life of ease or does work and domestic life dwell together? </span>
  <a data-text-id="815480462230219111" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer151075442" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17034256102900932472">I very much enjoy the work of Wendell Berry. His sensibility and point of view on the current state of humanity and the planet is a good one, and many fine poems originate there. He does enjoy wordplay to a great extent and not always to the benefit of his lines, but when that wordplay works, there are few who can best him. His "Manifesto: The Mad Farmer Liberation Front" is, for my money, the best poem in the collection, but there are quieter and deeper poems that are also impressive: "Another </span>
  <span id="freeText17034256102900932472" style="display:none">I very much enjoy the work of Wendell Berry. His sensibility and point of view on the current state of humanity and the planet is a good one, and many fine poems originate there. He does enjoy wordplay to a great extent and not always to the benefit of his lines, but when that wordplay works, there are few who can best him. His "Manifesto: The Mad Farmer Liberation Front" is, for my money, the best poem in the collection, but there are quieter and deeper poems that are also impressive: "Another Descent," "To Know the Dark," "The Snake," "Enriching the Earth," and "Traveling at Home" are my favorites. It is interesting that for me, most of the poems I liked best were selected from Farming: A Handbook on pages 103 to 140. All in all, if you are a reader, Wendell Berry can awaken your best sensibilities about living on the land and if you are a writer of poems yourself, he can give you pointers and add to your chops.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17034256102900932472" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer27401904" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8319619813568075836">Besides being a skilled novelist, Berry is also a consummate poet. This collection includes many beautiful pieces, including my favorites, <i>The Wild Geese</i> and <i>Manifesto: the Mad Farmer Liberation Front</i>. I carry this book with me every day in my backpack, along with another Berry collection, <i>The Long-Legged House</i>, a collection of essays, and I have frequently picked up this poetry collection to pass some free time. If you like poetry at all, give this a try.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer779403513757074791">I like his poems, and a lot of them remind me of Robert Frost in style and composition, except for the overtly political ones. My two favorites are "Boone" and "We Who Prayed and Wept." <br/><br/>With poetry, (and art), if I like it, that usually means I understand the poem and I didn't have to work to hard at "getting" it. Maybe that makes me a barbarian or a dummy; I like Berry's poems because they're simple and they shouldn't lend themselves to pretentious interpretations. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Graziella" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6494815-graziella"><img alt="Graziella" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1532791148p2/6494815.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/247421745?book_show_action=true">Dec 15, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Graziella" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Graziella" href="/user/show/6494815-graziella">Graziella</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6494815-graziella?shelf=top-shelf">top-shelf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer247421745" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16027126027383835027">Wendell Berry is a national treasure, his quiet gaze illuminating for us the sudden poetry in a flock of geese on the wing, and highlighting how the plowed earth in spring is Heaven's richest gift. Just reading two lines from his perspectives calms and separates a body from its contracted, multi-tasking anxiety, ushering us to a better, slower, more grateful and reflective way of being human.</span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7302368-bradley?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1407361982" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3466862082049206668">Wendell Berry has influenced my thinking and my writing probably more than any other person I HAVEN"T MET. His poetry has a hallowed quality, a reverent voice. I will refer back to the musings and commentary and observations for many years to come. As my father put it, "he understands the connection between an inner life and an outer life."</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('49N4GPhXCYmQd/cYrNC09/OviqCDQ/35XlshliTIAAyHMDaL+bOToEBK08C3aID0AQTgtqTXI1YPEe5seKlbxg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('0aMtE00khA/Q/O0XUg9xYgGDxgHQixA51ppWfaHpTMW1QGOATMAeJgDByc9Jt0Vh8yisF/cfzpaH0JmH/YgXDw==')})
  }

<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13552345-sowing-seeds-in-the-desert"><img alt="Sowing Seeds in the Desert: Natural Farming, Global Restoration, and Ultimate Food Security" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1332132922l/13552345._SX98_.jpg" />

This present condition of global trauma is not \"n<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText3803514562686207635\" style=\"display:none\">The earth is in great peril, due to the corporatization of agriculture, the rising climate crisis, and the ever-increasing levels of global poverty, starvation, and desertification on a massive scale. This present condition of global trauma is not &quot;natural,&quot; but a result of humanity\'s destructive actions. And, according to Masanobu Fukuoka, it is reversible. We need to change not only our methods of earth stewardship, but also the very way we think about the relationship between human beings and nature.\n\nFukuoka grew up on a farm on the island of Shikoku in Japan. As a young man he worked as a customs inspector for plants going into and out of the country. This was in the 1930s when science seemed poised to create a new world of abundance and leisure, when people fully believed they could improve upon nature by applying scientific methods and thereby reap untold rewards. While working there, Fukuoka had an insight that changed his life forever. He returned to his home village and applied this insight to developing a revolutionary new way of farming that he believed would be of great benefit to society. This method, which he called &quot;natural farming,&quot; involved working with, not in opposition to, nature.\n\nFukuoka\'s inspiring and internationally best-selling book, The One-Straw Revolution was first published in English in 1978. In this book, Fukuoka described his philosophy of natural farming and why he came to farm the way he did. One-Straw was a huge success in the West, and spoke directly to the growing movement of organic farmers and activists seeking a new way of life. For years after its publication, Fukuoka traveled around the world spreading his teachings and developing a devoted following of farmers seeking to get closer to the truth of nature.\n\nSowing Seeds in the Desert, a summation of those years of travel and research, is Fukuoka\'s last major work-and perhaps his most important. Fukuoka spent years working with people and organizations in Africa, India, Southeast Asia, Europe, and the United States, to prove that you could, indeed, grow food and regenerate forests with very little irrigation in the most desolate of places. Only by greening the desert, he said, would the world ever achieve true food security.\n\nThis revolutionary book presents Fukuoka\'s plan to rehabilitate the deserts of the world using natural farming, including practical solutions for feeding a growing human population, rehabilitating damaged landscapes, reversing the spread of desertification, and providing a deep understanding of the relationship between human beings and nature. Fukuoka\'s message comes right at the time when people around the world seem to have lost their frame of reference, and offers us a way forward.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"3803514562686207635\" Two hundred years later, Joy Harjo returns to her family’s lands and opens a dialogue with history. In An American Sunrise, Harjo finds blessings in the abundance of her homeland and confronts the site where her people, and other indigenous families, essentially disappeared. From her memory of her mother’s death, to her beginnings in the native rights movement, to the fresh road with her beloved, Harjo’s personal life intertwines with tribal histories to create a space for renewed beginnings. Her poems sing of beauty and survival, illuminating a spirituality that connects her to her ancestors and thrums with the quiet anger of living in the ruins of injustice. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer8991198757119479007">Wendell Berry is a conservationist, farmer, essayist, novelist, professor of English and poet. He was born August 5, 1934 in Henry County, Kentucky where he now lives on a farm. The New York Times has called Berry the "prophet of rural America."</span>

</script>
